</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Aaron</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about Aaron the <a href="/wiki/Kohen" title="Kohen">priest</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Aaron_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Aaron (disambiguation)">Aaron (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em">

<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>, <b>Aaron</b> <sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[note 1]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <big>אַהֲרֹן</big>; <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ær/ 'arr' in 'marry'">ær</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɛər/ 'are' in 'bare'">ɛər</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> was the elder brother of <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">Prophet</a> of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>. <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> literature, which also considers Aaron a Prophet of God, treats Aaron as being junior to Moses.<sup id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C7.7C103.7Ce.3D156.7Cs.3Dns_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C7.7C103.7Ce.3D156.7Cs.3Dns-7">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C19.7C41.7Ce.3D53.7Cs.3Dns_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C19.7C41.7Ce.3D53.7Cs.3Dns-8">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C20.7C9.7Ce.3D98.7Cs.3Dns_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C20.7C9.7Ce.3D98.7Cs.3Dns-9">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C28.7C34.7Cs.3Dns_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C28.7C34.7Cs.3Dns-10">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-harvnb.7CIbn_Hisham.7C1967.7Cp.3D604.3B_.C2.A7.3D897_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harvnb.7CIbn_Hisham.7C1967.7Cp.3D604.3B_.C2.A7.3D897-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Knowledge of Aaron, along with his brother Moses, comes exclusively from religious texts, such as the Bible and <a href="/wiki/Qur%E2%80%99an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur’an">Qur’an</a>. The Hebrew Bible relates that, unlike Moses, who grew up in the Egyptian royal court, Aaron and his elder sister <a href="/wiki/Miriam" title="Miriam">Miriam</a> remained with their <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kinsmen</a> in the eastern border-land of Egypt (<a href="/wiki/Land_of_Goshen" title="Land of Goshen">Goshen</a>). When Moses first confronted the Egyptian king about the Israelites, Aaron served as his brother's spokesman ("prophet") to the Pharaoh.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> Part of the Law (Torah) that Moses received from God at Sinai granted Aaron the priesthood for himself and his male descendants, and he became the first <a href="/wiki/High_Priest_(Judaism)" class="mw-redirect" title="High Priest (Judaism)">High Priest</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Aaron died before the Israelites crossed the North Jordan river and he was buried on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Hor" title="Mount Hor">Mount Hor</a> (Numbers 33:39;<sup id="cite_ref-jd3_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd3-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bibleref.7CNumbers.7C20:22.7C29.7CKJV_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref.7CNumbers.7C20:22.7C29.7CKJV-15">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bibleref.7CNumbers.7C33:38.7C39.7CKJV_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref.7CNumbers.7C33:38.7C39.7CKJV-16">[15]</a></sup> Deuteronomy 10:6 says he died and was buried at Moserah).<sup id="cite_ref-jd3_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd3-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> Aaron is also mentioned in the New Testament of the Bible.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Account_in_the_Hebrew_Bible">Account in the Hebrew Bible</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Account in the Hebrew Bible">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Book of Exodus</a>, Aaron first functioned as <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>' assistant. Because Moses complained that he could not speak well, God appointed Aaron as Moses' "prophet" (Exodus 4:10-17; 7:1).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[note 2]</a></sup> At the command of <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>, he let his rod turn into a snake.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> Then he stretched out his <a href="/wiki/Aaron%27s_rod" title="Aaron's rod">rod</a> in order to bring on the first three plagues.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> After that, Moses tended to act and speak for himself.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the journey in the wilderness, Aaron was not always prominent or active. At the battle with Amalek, he was chosen with <a href="/wiki/Hur_(Bible)" title="Hur (Bible)">Hur</a> to support the hand of Moses that held the "rod of God".<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> When the revelation was given to Moses at Mount Sinai, he headed the elders of Israel who accompanied Moses on the way to the summit. While <a href="/wiki/Joshua" title="Joshua">Joshua</a> went with Moses to the top, however, Aaron and Hur remained below to look after the people.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> From here on in Exodus, Leviticus and Numbers, Joshua appears in the role of Moses' assistant while Aaron functions instead as the first high priest.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Priest">High Priest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: High Priest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The books of Exodus, Leviticus and Numbers maintain that Aaron received from God a monopoly over the priesthood for himself and his male descendants (Exodus 28:1). The family of Aaron had the exclusive right and responsibility to make offerings on the altar to the God of Israel. The rest of his tribe, the <a href="/wiki/Levite" title="Levite">Levites</a>, were given subordinate responsibilities within the sanctuary (Numbers 3). Moses anointed and consecrated Aaron and his sons to the priesthood, and arrayed them in the robes of office (Leviticus 8; cf. Exodus 28-29). He also related to them God's detailed instructions for performing their duties while the rest of the Israelites listened (Leviticus 1-7, 11-27). Aaron and his successors as high priest were given control over the <a href="/wiki/Urim_and_Thummim" title="Urim and Thummim">Urim and Thummim</a> by which the will of God could be determined (Exodus 28:30).<sup id="cite_ref-Olson_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olson-1">[1]</a></sup> God commissioned the Aaronide priests to distinguish the holy from the common and the clean from the unclean, and to teach the divine laws (the Torah) to the Israelites (Leviticus 10:10-11). The priests were also commissioned to bless the people (Numbers 6:22-27).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> When Aaron completed the altar offerings for the first time and, with Moses, "blessed the people: and the glory of the L<span class="smallcaps" style="font-size:83%">ORD</span> appeared unto all the people: And there came a fire out from before the L<span class="smallcaps" style="font-size:83%">ORD</span>, and consumed upon the altar the burnt offering and the fat [which] when all the people saw, they shouted, and fell on their faces" (Leviticus 9:23-24).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> In this way, the institution of the Aaronide priesthood was established.<sup id="cite_ref-phod_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phod-36">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In later books of the Old Testament, Aaron and his kin are not mentioned very often except in literature dating to the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_Captivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Babylonian Captivity">Babylonian Exile</a> and later. The books of Judges, Samuel and Kings mention priests and Levites, but do not mention the Aaronides in particular. The book of <a href="/wiki/Ezekiel" title="Ezekiel">Ezekiel</a>, which devotes much attention to priestly matters, calls the priestly upper class the <a href="/wiki/Zadokites" class="mw-redirect" title="Zadokites">Zadokites</a> after one of King David's priests.<sup id="cite_ref-Olson_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olson-1">[1]</a></sup> It does reflect a two-tier priesthood with the Levites in subordinate position. A two-tier hierarchy of Aaronides and Levites appears in Ezra, Nehemiah and Chronicles. As a result, many historians think that Aaronide families did not control the priesthood in pre-exilic Israel. What is clear is that high priests claiming Aaronide descent dominated the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple_period" title="Second Temple period">Second Temple period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> Most scholars think the Pentateuch reached its final form early in this period, which may account for Aaron's prominence in Exodus, Leviticus and Numbers.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_conflicts">Leadership conflicts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Leadership conflicts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aaron plays a leading role in several stories of conflicts over leadership during Israel's wilderness wanderings. During the prolonged absence of Moses on Mount Sinai, the people provoked Aaron to make a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Calf" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Calf">Golden Calf</a> as a visible image of the divinity who had delivered them from Egypt (Exodus 32:1-6).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> This incident nearly caused God to destroy the Israelites for their unfaithfulness to the covenant (Exodus 32:10). Moses successfully intervened, but then led the loyal Levites in executing many of the culprits; a plague afflicted those who were left (Exodus 32:25-35).<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> Aaron, however, escaped punishment for his role in the affair, because of the intercession of Moses according to Deuteronomy 9:20.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> Later retellings of this story almost always excuse Aaron for his role.<sup id="cite_ref-Watts_2011_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watts_2011-41">[39]</a></sup> For example, in rabbinic sources<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> and in the Qur'an, Aaron was not the idol-maker and upon Moses' return begged his pardon because he felt mortally threatened by the Israelites (Quran 7:142-152).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the day of Aaron's consecration, his oldest sons, <a href="/wiki/Nadab_and_Abihu" title="Nadab and Abihu">Nadab and Abihu</a>, were burned up by divine fire because they offered "strange" incense (Leviticus 10:1-3).<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup> Most interpreters think this story reflects a conflict between priestly families some time in Israel's past. Others argue that the story simply shows what can happen if the priests do not follow God's instructions given through Moses.<sup id="cite_ref-Watts_2011_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watts_2011-41">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Pentateuch generally depicts the siblings, Moses, Aaron, and Miriam, as the leaders of Israel after the Exodus, a view also reflected in the biblical book of Micah.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> Numbers 12, however, reports that on one occasion, Aaron and Miriam complained about Moses' exclusive claim to be the L<span class="smallcaps" style="font-size:83%">ORD</span>'s prophet.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> Their presumption was rebuffed by God who affirmed Moses' uniqueness as the one with whom the L<span class="smallcaps" style="font-size:83%">ORD</span> spoke face to face. Miriam was punished with skin disease (<i><a href="/wiki/Tzaraath" title="Tzaraath">leprous</a></i>), that turned her skin white. Aaron pleaded with Moses to intercede for her, and Miriam, after seven days' quarantine, was healed. Aaron once again escaped any retribution.</p>
<p>According to Numbers 16-17, a Levite named <a href="/wiki/Korah" title="Korah">Korah</a> led many in challenging Aaron's exclusive claim to the priesthood. When the rebels were punished by being swallowed up by the earth, (Numbers 16:25-35),<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> Eleazar, the son of Aaron, was commissioned to take charge of the censers of the dead priests. And when a plague broke out among the people who had sympathized with the rebels, Aaron, at the command of Moses, took his censer and stood between the living and the dead till the plague abated (Numbers 17:1-15, 16:36-50).<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hirschvogel_The_Blossoming_of_Aarons_Rod.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hirschvogel_The_Blossoming_of_Aarons_Rod.jpg/220px-Hirschvogel_The_Blossoming_of_Aarons_Rod.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hirschvogel_The_Blossoming_of_Aarons_Rod.jpg/330px-Hirschvogel_The_Blossoming_of_Aarons_Rod.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hirschvogel_The_Blossoming_of_Aarons_Rod.jpg/440px-Hirschvogel_The_Blossoming_of_Aarons_Rod.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1129" data-file-height="895" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hirschvogel_The_Blossoming_of_Aarons_Rod.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Blossoming of Aaron's Rod, etching by <a href="/wiki/Augustin_Hirschvogel" title="Augustin Hirschvogel">Augustin Hirschvogel</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To emphasize the validity of the Levites' claim to the offerings and tithes of the Israelites, Moses collected a rod from the leaders of each tribe in Israel and laid the twelve rods over night in the tent of meeting. The next morning, Aaron's rod was found to have budded and blossomed and produced ripe almonds (Numbers 17:8).<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> The following chapter then details the distinction between Aaron's family and the rest of the Levites: while all the Levites (and only Levites) were devoted to the care of the sanctuary, charge of its interior and the altar was committed to the <a href="/wiki/Kohen" title="Kohen">Aaronites</a> alone (Numbers 18:1-7).<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aaron, like Moses, was not permitted to enter Canaan with the Israelites<sup id="cite_ref-jd3_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd3-14">[13]</a></sup> because the two brothers showed impatience at <a href="/wiki/Meribah" title="Meribah">Meribah</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kadesh_(Israel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kadesh (Israel)">Kadesh</a>) in the last year of the desert pilgrimage (Numbers 20:12-13),<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> when Moses brought water out of a rock to quench the people's thirst. Though they had been commanded to speak to the rock, Moses struck it with the staff twice, which was construed as displaying a lack of deference to the L<span class="smallcaps" style="font-size:83%">ORD</span> (Numbers 20:7-11).<sup id="cite_ref-jd3_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd3-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are two accounts of the death of Aaron in the Pentateuch.<sup id="cite_ref-jd3_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd3-14">[13]</a></sup> Numbers says that soon after the incident at Meribah, Aaron with his son Eleazar and Moses ascended Mount Hor. There Moses stripped Aaron of his priestly garments and transferred them to Eleazar. Aaron died on the summit of the mountain, and the people mourned for him thirty days (Numbers 20:22-29; compare 33:38-39).<sup id="cite_ref-jd3_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd3-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bibleref.7CNumbers.7C20:22.7C29.7CKJV_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref.7CNumbers.7C20:22.7C29.7CKJV-15">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bibleref.7CNumbers.7C33:38.7C39.7CKJV_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref.7CNumbers.7C33:38.7C39.7CKJV-16">[15]</a></sup> The other account is found in Deuteronomy 10:6, where Aaron died at Moserah and was buried.<sup id="cite_ref-jd3_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd3-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> There is a significant amount of travel between these two points, as the itinerary in Numbers 33:31–37 records seven stages between Moseroth (Mosera) and Mount Hor.<sup id="cite_ref-jd3_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd3-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> Aaron was 123 at the time of his death.<sup id="cite_ref-Colliers_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colliers-58">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_tree">Family tree</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Family tree">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historicity">Historicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Historicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Moses#Historicity" title="Moses">Moses §&#160;Historicity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus#Historicity" title="The Exodus">The Exodus §&#160;Historicity</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Descendants">Descendants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Descendants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Aaron married <a href="/wiki/Elisheba" title="Elisheba">Elisheba</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Amminadab" title="Amminadab">Amminadab</a> and sister of <a href="/wiki/Nahshon" title="Nahshon">Nahshon</a> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Exodus</a> 6:23) of the tribe of Judah. The sons of Aaron were <a href="/wiki/Eleazar" title="Eleazar">Eleazar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ithamar" title="Ithamar">Ithamar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nadab_and_Abihu" title="Nadab and Abihu">Nadab and Abihu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[note 3]</a></sup> A descendant of Aaron is an Aaronite, or <a href="/wiki/Kohen" title="Kohen">Kohen</a>, meaning Priest.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup> Any non-Aaronic <a href="/wiki/Levite" title="Levite">Levite</a>—i.e., descended from Levi but not from Aaron<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup>—assisted the Levitical priests of the family of Aaron in the care of the tabernacle; later of the temple.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[note 4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Gospel of Luke</a> records that both <a href="/wiki/Zechariah_(priest)" title="Zechariah (priest)">Zechariah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_(biblical_figure)" title="Elizabeth (biblical figure)">Elizabeth</a> and therefore their son <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> were descendants of Aaron.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aaron_in_religious_traditions">Aaron in religious traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Aaron in religious traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_rabbinic_literature">Jewish rabbinic literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Jewish rabbinic literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The older prophets and prophetical writers beheld in their priests the representatives of a religious form inferior to the prophetic truth; men without the spirit of God and lacking the will-power requisite to resist the multitude in its idolatrous proclivities.<sup id="cite_ref-jd2_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd2-66">[62]</a></sup> Thus Aaron, the first priest, ranks below Moses: he is his mouthpiece, and the executor of the will of God revealed through Moses, although it is pointed out<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup> that it is said fifteen times in the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Pentateuch</a> that "the Lord spoke to Moses <i>and</i> Aaron." Under the influence of the priesthood that shaped the destinies of the nation under <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian</a> rule, a different ideal of the priest was formed, according to <a href="/wiki/Malachi" title="Malachi">Malachi</a> 2:4–7, and the prevailing tendency was to place Aaron on a footing equal with Moses.<sup id="cite_ref-jd2_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd2-66">[62]</a></sup> "At times Aaron, and at other times Moses, is mentioned first in Scripture—this is to show that they were of equal rank," says <a href="/wiki/Mekhilta_of_Rabbi_Ishmael" title="Mekhilta of Rabbi Ishmael">Mekilta</a>,(12) who strongly implies this when introducing in his record of renowned men the glowing description of Aaron's ministration.<sup id="cite_ref-jd2_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd2-66">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>In fulfilment of the promise of peaceful life, symbolized by the pouring of oil upon his head (<a href="/wiki/Leviticus_Rabbah" title="Leviticus Rabbah">Leviticus Rabbah</a> x., <a href="/wiki/Midrash_Tehillim" title="Midrash Tehillim">Midrash Teh.</a> cxxxiii. 1), Aaron's death, as described in the <a href="/wiki/Haggadah" title="Haggadah">Haggadah</a>, was of a wonderful tranquility.<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> Accompanied by Moses, his brother, and by Eleazar, his son, Aaron went to the summit of Mount Hor, where the rock suddenly opened before him and a beautiful cave lit by a lamp presented itself to his view. "Take off thy priestly raiment and place it upon thy son Eleazar!" said Moses; "and then follow me."<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> Aaron did as commanded; and they entered the cave, where was prepared a bed around which angels stood. "Go lie down upon thy bed, my brother," Moses continued; and Aaron obeyed without a murmur.<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> Then his soul departed as if by a kiss from God. The cave closed behind Moses as he left; and he went down the hill with Eleazar, with garments rent, and crying: "Alas, Aaron, my brother! thou, the pillar of supplication of Israel!"<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> When the Israelites cried in bewilderment, "Where is Aaron?" angels were seen carrying Aaron's bier through the air.<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> A voice was then heard saying: "The law of truth was in his mouth, and iniquity was not found on his lips: he walked with me in righteousness, and brought many back from sin" (Malachi 2:6).<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> He died, according to <a href="/wiki/Seder_Olam_Rabbah" title="Seder Olam Rabbah">Seder Olam Rabbah</a> ix., R. H. 2, 3a, on the first of Ab."<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> The pillar of cloud which proceeded in front of Israel's camp disappeared at Aaron's death (see Seder 'Olam, ix. and R. H. 2b-3a).<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> The seeming contradiction between Numbers 20:22 et seq. and Deuteronomy 10:6 is solved by the rabbis in the following manner: Aaron's death on Mount Hor was marked by the defeat of the people in a war with the king of Arad, in consequence of which the Israelites fled, marching seven stations backward to Mosera, where they performed the rites of mourning for Aaron; wherefore it is said: "There [at Mosera] died Aaron."<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[note 5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rabbis also dwell with special laudation on the brotherly sentiment which united Aaron and Moses. When the latter was appointed ruler and Aaron high priest, neither betrayed any jealousy; instead they rejoiced in one another's greatness. When Moses at first declined to go to Pharaoh, saying: "O my Lord, send, I pray thee, by the hand of him whom thou wilt send" (Exodus 4:13), he was unwilling to deprive Aaron, his brother, of the high position the latter had held for so many years; but the Lord reassured him, saying: "Behold, when he seeth thee, he will be glad in his heart" (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/e/et/et0204.htm#14">Exodus 4:14</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> Indeed, Aaron was to find his reward, says <a href="/wiki/Shimon_bar_Yochai" class="mw-redirect" title="Shimon bar Yochai">Shimon bar Yochai</a>; for that heart which had leaped with joy over his younger brother's rise to glory greater than his was decorated with the <a href="/wiki/Urim_and_Thummim" title="Urim and Thummim">Urim and Thummim</a>, which were to "be upon Aaron's heart when he goeth in before the Lord" (<a href="/wiki/Canticles_Rabbah" class="mw-redirect" title="Canticles Rabbah">Canticles Rabbah</a> i. 10).<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> Moses and Aaron met in gladness of heart, kissing each other as true brothers (Exodus 4:27; compare <a href="/wiki/Song_of_Solomon" class="mw-redirect" title="Song of Solomon">Song of Songs</a> 8:1), and of them it is written: "Behold how good and how pleasant [it is] for brethren to dwell together in unity!" (Psalms 133:1).<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup> Of them it is said: "Mercy and truth are met together; righteousness and peace have kissed [each other]" (Psalms 85:10); for Moses stood for righteousness, according to Deuteronomy 33:21, and Aaron for peace, according to <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/e/et/et2402.htm#6">Malachi 2:6</a>. Again, mercy was personified in Aaron, according to Deuteronomy 33:8, and truth in Moses, according to Numbers 12:7 .<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Moses poured the oil of anointment upon the head of Aaron, Aaron modestly shrank back and said: "Who knows whether I have not cast some blemish upon this sacred oil so as to forfeit this high office." Then the <a href="/wiki/Shekhinah" title="Shekhinah">Shekhinah</a> spoke the words: "Behold the precious ointment upon the head, that ran down upon the beard of Aaron, that even went down to the skirts of his garment, is as pure as the dew of Hermon" (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=Psalms+133:2–3:2&amp;version=nrsv">Psalm 133:2-3</a>) .<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Tanhuma" title="Tanhuma">Tanhuma</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup> Aaron's activity as a prophet began earlier than that of Moses.<sup id="cite_ref-jd2_66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd2-66">[62]</a></sup> Hillel held Aaron up as an example, saying: "Be of the disciples of Aaron, loving peace and pursuing peace; love your fellow creatures and draw them nigh unto the Law!"<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[68]</a></sup> This is further illustrated by the tradition preserved in Abot de-Rabbi Natan 12, <a href="/wiki/Sanhedrin_(tractate)" title="Sanhedrin (tractate)">Sanhedrin</a> 6b, and elsewhere, according to which Aaron was an ideal priest of the people, far more beloved for his kindly ways than was Moses.<sup id="cite_ref-jd3_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd3-14">[13]</a></sup> While Moses was stern and uncompromising, brooking no wrong, Aaron went about as peacemaker, reconciling man and wife when he saw them estranged, or a man with his neighbor when they quarreled, and winning evil-doers back into the right way by his friendly intercourse.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup> The mourning of the people at Aaron's death was greater, therefore, than at that of Moses; for whereas, when Aaron died the whole <a href="/wiki/House_of_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Israel">house of Israel</a> wept, including the women, (Numbers 20:29)<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup> Moses was bewailed by "the sons of Israel" only (Deuteronomy 34:8).<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[71]</a></sup> Even in the making of the Golden Calf the rabbis find extenuating circumstances for Aaron.<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[72]</a></sup> His fortitude and silent submission to the will of God on the loss of his two sons are referred to as an excellent example to men how to glorify God in the midst of great affliction.<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup> Especially significant are the words represented as being spoken by God after the princes of the <a href="/wiki/Israelites#Biblical_Israel" title="Israelites">Twelve Tribes</a> had brought their dedication offerings into the newly reared Tabernacle: "Say to thy brother Aaron: Greater than the gifts of the princes is thy gift; for thou art called upon to kindle the light, and, while the sacrifices shall last only as long as the Temple lasts, thy light shall last forever."<sup id="cite_ref-jd4_68-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jd4-68">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aaron-icon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Aaron-icon.jpg/220px-Aaron-icon.jpg" width="220" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Aaron-icon.jpg/330px-Aaron-icon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/Aaron-icon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="416" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aaron-icon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Russian_icon" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian icon">Russian icon</a> of Aaron (18th century, <a href="/wiki/Iconostasis" title="Iconostasis">Iconostasis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kizhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Kizhi">Kizhi</a> <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karelia" title="Karelia">Karelia</a>, Russia).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Church">Maronite</a> churches, Aaron is <a href="/wiki/Veneration" title="Veneration">venerated</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a> whose <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast day</a> is shared with his brother Moses and celebrated on September 4. (Those churches that follow the traditional <a href="/wiki/Julian_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Julian Calendar">Julian Calendar</a> celebrate this day on September 17 of the modern <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregorian Calendar">Gregorian Calendar</a>). Aaron is also commemorated with other Old Testament saints on the Sunday of the Holy Fathers, the Sunday before <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>.</p>
<p>Aaron is commemorated as one of the Holy Forefathers in the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_Saints_(Armenian_Apostolic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Calendar of Saints (Armenian Apostolic Church)">Calendar of Saints of the Armenian Apostolic Church</a> on July 30. He is commemorated on July 1 in the modern Latin calendar and in the Syriac Calendar.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Aaronic_priesthood_(LDS_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aaronic priesthood (LDS Church)">Aaronic</a> order is the lesser order of priesthood, comprising the grades (from lowest to highest) of deacon, teacher, and priest. The chief office of the Aaronic priesthood is the presiding bishopric;<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup> the head of the priesthood is the bishop. Each ward includes a quorum of one or more of each office of the Aaronic priesthood.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Christ" title="Community of Christ">Community of Christ</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_(Community_of_Christ)#Aaronic_Order" title="Priesthood (Community of Christ)">Aaronic order of priesthood</a> is regarded as an appendage to the <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_(Community_of_Christ)#Melchisedec_Order" title="Priesthood (Community of Christ)">Melchisedec order</a>, and consists of the priesthood offices of deacon, teacher, and priest. While differing in responsibilities, these offices, along with those of the Melchisidec order, are regarded as equal before God.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Moses in Islam</a></div>
<p>Aaron (<a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>: هارون, <i>Hārūn</i>) is also mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Qur%E2%80%99an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur’an">Qur’an</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophets of Islam">prophet</a> of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup> The Qur’an praises Aaron repeatedly, calling him a "believing servant"<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup> as well as one who was "guided"<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[79]</a></sup> and one of the "victors".<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup> Aaron is important in <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> for his role in the events of <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">the Exodus</a>, in which, according to the Qur’an and <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a>, he preached with his elder brother, <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Moses</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C19.7C41.7Ce.3D53.7Cs.3Dns_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C19.7C41.7Ce.3D53.7Cs.3Dns-8">[7]</a></sup> to the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh_of_the_Exodus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharaoh of the Exodus">Pharaoh of the Exodus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup> Aaron's significance in Islam, however, is not limited to his role as the helper of Moses. Islamic tradition also accords Aaron the role of a <a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">patriarch</a>, as tradition records that the priestly descent came through Aaron's lineage, which included the entire <a href="/wiki/Al-Imran" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Imran">House of Amran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[note 6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[note 7]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aaron_in_the_Qur.E2.80.99an">Aaron in the Qur’an</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Aaron in the Qur’an">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks collapsible" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%">
<tr>
<th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;font-size:88%; line-height:200%;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basmala.svg" class="image" title="Bismillahir Rahmanir Rahim"><img alt="Bismillahir Rahmanir Rahim" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basmala.svg/200px-Basmala.svg.png" width="200" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basmala.svg/300px-Basmala.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basmala.svg/400px-Basmala.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="160" /></a> <span style="font-size:110%;"><span style="font-weight:normal;">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Islam" title="Category:Islam">a series</a> on <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a></span></span><br />
<span style="font-size:220%;"><a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">Islamic prophets</a></span></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Anbiya.png" class="image" title="Anbiya"><img alt="Anbiya" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Al-Anbiya.png/140px-Al-Anbiya.png" width="140" height="105" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Al-Anbiya.png/210px-Al-Anbiya.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Al-Anbiya.png/280px-Al-Anbiya.png 2x" data-file-width="1518" data-file-height="1143" /></a></td>
</tr>

<td class="hlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">

<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Dhul-Kifl" title="Dhul-Kifl">Dhul-Kifl</a> <small>(<a href="/wiki/Ezekiel" title="Ezekiel">Ezekiel</a>)</small></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Shuaib" title="Shuaib">Shuʿayb</a> <small>(<a href="/wiki/Jethro_(Bible)" title="Jethro (Bible)">Jethro</a>)</small></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Mūsā</a>* <small>(<a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>)</small></span></li>

<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Jonah#Jonah_in_Islam" title="Jonah">Yūnus</a> <small>(<a href="/wiki/Jonah" title="Jonah">Jonah</a>)</small></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Elijah#Elijah_in_Islam" title="Elijah">ʾIlyās</a> <small>(<a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elijah</a>)</small></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Elisha#In_Islam" title="Elisha">Alyasaʿ</a> <small>(<a href="/wiki/Elisha" title="Elisha">Elisha</a>)</small></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Zechariah_(priest)#In_Islam" title="Zechariah (priest)">Zakarīya</a> <small>(<a href="/wiki/Zechariah_(priest)" title="Zechariah (priest)">Zechariah</a>)</small></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist#Islam" title="John the Baptist">Yaḥyā</a> <small>(<a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John</a>)</small></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">ʿĪsā</a>* <small>(<a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>)</small></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muḥammad</a>* <small>(<a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>)</small></span></li>
</ul>
</div>

<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;"><a href="/wiki/File:Allah-green.svg" class="image"><img alt="Allah-green.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/15px-Allah-green.svg.png" width="15" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/23px-Allah-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/31px-Allah-green.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="206" data-file-height="215" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam" title="Portal:Islam">Islam portal</a></td>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Islamic_prophets" title="Template:Islamic prophets"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Islamic_prophets&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template talk:Islamic prophets (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Islamic_prophets&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The Qur’an contains numerous references to Aaron, both by name and without name. It says that he was a descendant of Abraham (Qur'an 4: 163) and makes it clear that both he and Moses were sent together to warn the Pharaoh about God's punishment (Qur'an 10: 75). It further adds that Moses had earlier prayed to God to strengthen his own ministry with Aaron (Qur'an 20: 29-30) and that Aaron helped Moses as he too was a prophet (Qur'an 19: 53), and very eloquent in matters of speech and discourse (Qur'an 28: 34). The Qur'an adds that both Moses and Aaron were entrusted to establish places of dwelling for the Israelites in Egypt, and to convert those houses into places of worship for God (Qur'an 10: 87).</p>
<p>The incident of the Golden Calf as it is narrated in the Qur'an paints Aaron in a positive light. The Qur'an says that Aaron was entrusted the leadership of Israel while Moses was up on <i>Tur Sina’</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">طُـور سِـيـنـاء</span>‎‎, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sinai" title="Mount Sinai">Mount Sinai</a>) for a period of forty days (Qur'an 7: 142).<sup id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C7.7C103.7Ce.3D156.7Cs.3Dns_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C7.7C103.7Ce.3D156.7Cs.3Dns-7">[6]</a></sup> It adds that Aaron tried his best to stop the worship of the Golden Calf, which was built not by Aaron but by a wicked man by the name of '<a href="/wiki/Samiri_(Islamic_figure)" title="Samiri (Islamic figure)">As-Samiri</a>' (Qur'an 19: 50). When Moses returned from Mount Sinai, he rebuked Aaron for allowing the worship of the idol, to which Aaron pleaded with Moses to not blame him when he had no role in its construction (Qur'an 7: 150).<sup id="cite_ref-Cite_quran.7C7.7C103.7Ce.3D156.7Cs.3Dns_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_quran.7C7.7C103.7Ce.3D156.7Cs.3Dns-7">[6]</a></sup> The Qur'an then adds that Moses here lamented the sins of Israel, and said that he only had power over himself and Aaron (Qur'an 5: 25).</p>
<p>Aaron is later commemorated in the Qur'an as one who had a "clear authority" (Qur'an 23: 45) and one who was "guided to the Right Path" (Qur'an 37: 118). It further adds that Aaron's memory was left for people who came after him (Qur'an 37: 119) and he is blessed by God along with his brother (Qur'an 37: 120). The Qur'an also says that people called <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">‘Isa</a>'s mother <a href="/wiki/Mary_in_Islam" title="Mary in Islam">Maryam</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">مَـرْيَـم</span>‎‎, <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Mary</a>) a "sister of Harun" (Qur'an 19: 28). Muslim scholars debated as to who exactly this "Harun" was in terms of his historical persona, with some saying that it was a reference to Aaron of the Exodus, and the term "sister" designating only a metaphorical or spiritual link between the two figures, all the more evident when Mary was a descendant of the priestly lineage of Aaron, while others held it to be another righteous man living at the time of <a href="/wiki/Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ">Christ</a> by the name of "Aaron". Most scholars have agreed to the former perspective, and have linked Mary spiritually with the actual sister of Aaron, her namesake <a href="/wiki/Miriam" title="Miriam">Miryam</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">مِـرْيَـم</span>‎‎, <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span lang="he" dir="rtl" xml:lang="he">מִרְיָם</span>‎‎),<sup id="cite_ref-RU2016_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RU2016-91">[84]</a></sup> whom she resembled in many ways. The Qur'an also narrates that, centuries later, when the <i>Tabut</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">تَـابـوت</span>‎‎, <a href="/wiki/Ark_of_the_Covenant" title="Ark of the Covenant">Ark of the Covenant</a>) returned to Israel, it contained "relics from the family of Moses and relics from the family of Aaron" (Qur'an 2: 248).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aaron_in_Muhammad.27s_time">Aaron in Muhammad's time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Aaron in Muhammad's time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, in many of his sayings, speaks of Aaron. In the event of the <a href="/wiki/Mi%27raj" class="mw-redirect" title="Mi'raj">Mi'raj</a>, his miraculous ascension through the Heavens, Muhammad is said to have encountered Aaron in the fifth heaven.<sup id="cite_ref-muslim_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muslim-92">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[86]</a></sup> According to old scholars, including <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hisham" title="Ibn Hisham">Ibn Hisham</a>, Muhammad, in particular, mentioned the beauty of Aaron when he encountered him in Heaven. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Lings" title="Martin Lings">Martin Lings</a>, in his biographical <i>Muhammad</i>, speaks of Muhammad's wonderment at seeing fellow prophets in their heavenly glory:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Of Joseph he said that his face had the splendour of the moon at its full, and that he had been endowed with no less than the half of all existing beauty. Yet this did not diminish Muhammad's wonderment at his brethren, and he mentioned in particular the great beauty of Aaron.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[88]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Aaron was also mentioned by Muhammad in likeness to ‘<a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a>. Muhammad had left ‘Ali to look after his family, but the hypocrites of the time begun to spread the rumor that the prophet found ‘Ali a burden and was relieved to be rid of his presence. ‘Ali, grieved at hearing this wicked taunt, told Muhammad what the local people were saying. In reply, the Prophet said: "They lie, I bade thee remain for the sake of what I had left behind me. So return and represent me in my family and in thine. Art thou not content, O ‘Ali, that thou should be unto me as Aaron was unto Moses, save that after me there is no prophet."<sup id="cite_ref-harvnb.7CIbn_Hisham.7C1967.7Cp.3D604.3B_.C2.A7.3D897_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harvnb.7CIbn_Hisham.7C1967.7Cp.3D604.3B_.C2.A7.3D897-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tomb_of_Aaron">Tomb of Aaron</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tomb of Aaron">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Aaron" title="Tomb of Aaron">Tomb of Aaron</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shrine_on_Mt_Aaron.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Shrine_on_Mt_Aaron.jpg/220px-Shrine_on_Mt_Aaron.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Shrine_on_Mt_Aaron.jpg/330px-Shrine_on_Mt_Aaron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Shrine_on_Mt_Aaron.jpg/440px-Shrine_on_Mt_Aaron.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shrine_on_Mt_Aaron.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 14th-century shrine built on top of the supposed grave of Aaron on Jabal Hārūn in <a href="/wiki/Petra" title="Petra">Petra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to Islamic tradition, the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Aaron" title="Tomb of Aaron">tomb of Aaron</a> is located on <i>Jabal Harun</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">جَـبـل هَـارون</span>‎‎, Mountain of Aaron), near <a href="/wiki/Petra" title="Petra">Petra</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[90]</a></sup> At 1,350.0&#160;m (4,429.1 feet) above sea-level, it is the highest peak in the area; and it is a place of great sanctity to the local people for here. A 14th-century <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluk</a> mosque stands here with its white dome visible from most areas in and around Petra.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baha.27i">Baha'i</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Baha'i">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although his father is described as both an apostle and a prophet, Aaron is merely described as a prophet. The Kitab-I-Iqan describes Imran as being his father.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_history">Art history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Art history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Aaron appears paired with Moses frequently in Jewish and Christian art, especially in the illustrations of manuscript and printed Bibles.<sup id="cite_ref-watt3_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watt3-100">[93]</a></sup> He can usually be distinguished by his priestly vestments, especially his turban or miter and jeweled breastplate. He frequently holds a censor or, sometimes, his flowering rod. (See <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Aaron" class="extiw" title="commons:Aaron">the "Aaron" category</a> at Wikimedia Commons.) Aaron also appears in scenes depicting the wilderness Tabernacle and its altar, as already in the third-century frescos in the <a href="/wiki/Dura-Europos_synagogue" title="Dura-Europos synagogue">synagogue</a> at <a href="/wiki/Dura-Europos" title="Dura-Europos">Dura-Europos</a> in Syria. An eleventh-century portable silver altar from <a href="/wiki/Fulda" title="Fulda">Fulda</a>, Germany depicts Aaron with his censor, and is located in the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mus%C3%A9e_National_de_l%E2%80%99Age_M%C3%A9di%C3%A9vale&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Musée National de l’Age Médiévale (page does not exist)">Musée National de l’Age Médiévale</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>. This is also how he appears in the frontispieces of early printed Passover Haggadot and occasionally in church sculptures. Aaron has rarely been the subject of portraits, such as those by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Anton_Kern&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Anton Kern (page does not exist)">Anton Kern</a> [1710–1747] and by <a href="/wiki/Pier_Francesco_Mola" title="Pier Francesco Mola">Pier Francesco Mola</a> [c. 1650].<sup id="cite_ref-Kline_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kline-101">[94]</a></sup> Christian artists sometimes portray Aaron as a prophet (Exod. 7:1) holding a scroll, as in a twelfth-century sculpture from the <a href="/wiki/Noyon_Cathedral" title="Noyon Cathedral">Cathedral of Noyon</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>, New York and often in Eastern Orthodox icons. Illustrations of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Calf" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Calf">Golden Calf</a> story usually include him as well—most notably in <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Poussin" title="Nicolas Poussin">Nicolas Poussin</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/The_Adoration_of_the_Golden_Calf" title="The Adoration of the Golden Calf">The Adoration of the Golden Calf</a>" (ca. 1633–34, <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery" title="National Gallery">National Gallery</a> London).<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[95]</a></sup> Finally, some artists interested in validating later priesthoods have painted the ordination of Aaron and his sons (Leviticus 8). <a href="/wiki/Harry_Anderson_(artist)" title="Harry Anderson (artist)">Harry Anderson</a>'s realistic portrayal is often reproduced in the literature of the Latter Day Saints.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[note 8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-watt3_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watt3-100">[93]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aaron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

(A third group followed neither.) Ironically, and likewise according to Samaritan sources, the high priests' line of the sons of Phineas died out in 1624 CE with the death of the 112th High Priest, Shlomyah ben Pinhas, at which time the priesthood was transferred to the sons of Itamar. See article Samaritan for list of High Priests from 1613 to 2004—the 131st high priest of the Samaritans is <a href="/wiki/Elazar_ben_Tsedaka_ben_Yitzhaq" title="Elazar ben Tsedaka ben Yitzhaq">Elazar ben Tsedaka ben Yitzhaq</a>. They led active lives and called 'doers of good.'"<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup></span></li>

<li>Aaron and Moses sent to Pharaoh: <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D23%3Averse%3D45">23:45</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D10%3Averse%3D75">10:75</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D10%3Averse%3D87">10:87</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D21%3Averse%3D48">21:48</a></li>

<td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2">
<div>Note: <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> believe that there were many prophets sent by <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> to mankind. The <a href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophets in Islam">Islamic prophets</a> above are only the ones mentioned by name in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>.</div>
